canbe10{_}98{_}Contents:  The death of Halpin Frayser The secret of Macarger's Gulch One summer night The moonlit road A diagnosis of death Moxon's master A tough tussle One of twins The haunted valley A jug of sirup Staley Fleming's hallucination A resumed identity Hazen's brigade A baby tramp The night-doings at "Deadman's" A story that is untrue Beyond the wall A psychological shipwreck The middle toe of the right foot John Mortonson's funeral The realm of the unreal John Bartine's watch A story by a physician The damned thing Haita the shepherd An inhabitant of Carcosa The Stranger    THE DEATH OF HALPIN FRAYSER    I  For by death is wrought greater change than hath been shown.
canbe10{_}99{_}Whereas in general the spirit that removed cometh back upon occasion, and is sometimes seen of those in flesh (appearing in the form of the body it bore) yet it hath happened that the veritable body without the spirit hath walked.
canbe10{_}100{_}And it is attested of those encountering who have lived to speak thereon that a lich so raised up hath no natural affection, nor remembrance thereof, but only hate.
canbe10{_}101{_}Also, it is known that some spirits which in life were benign become by death evil altogether.--Hali.
canbe10{_}102{_}One dark night in midsummer a man waking from a dreamless sleep in a forest lifted his head from the earth, and staring a few moments into the blackness, said:  "Catherine Larue."
canbe10{_}103{_}He said nothing more; no reason was known to him why he should have said so much.
canbe10{_}104{_}The man was Halpin Frayser.
canbe10{_}105{_}He lived in St. Helena, but where he lives now is uncertain, for he is dead.
canbe10{_}106{_}One who practices sleeping in the woods with nothing under him but the dry leaves and the damp earth, and nothing over him but the branches from which the leaves have fallen and the sky from which the earth has fallen, cannot hope for great longevity, and Frayser had already attained the age of thirty-two.
canbe10{_}107{_}There are persons in this world, millions of persons, and far and away the best persons, who regard that as a very advanced age.
canbe10{_}108{_}They are the children.
canbe10{_}109{_}To those who view the voyage of life from the port of departure the bark that has accomplished any considerable distance appears already in close approach to the farther shore.
canbe10{_}110{_}However, it is not certain that Halpin Frayser came to his death by exposure.
canbe10{_}111{_}He had been all day in the hills west of the Napa Valley, looking for doves and such small game as was in season.
canbe10{_}112{_}Late in the afternoon it had come on to be cloudy, and he had lost his bearings; and although he had only to go always downhill--everywhere the way to safety when one is lost--the absence of trails had so impeded him that he was overtaken by night while still in the forest.
canbe10{_}113{_}Unable in the darkness to penetrate the thickets of manzanita and other undergrowth, utterly bewildered and overcome with fatigue, he had lain down near the root of a large madrono and fallen into a dreamless sleep.
canbe10{_}114{_}It was hours later, in the very middle of the night, that one of God's mysterious messengers, gliding ahead of the incalculable host of his companions sweeping westward with the dawn line, pronounced the awakening word in the ear of the sleeper, who sat upright and spoke, he knew not why, a name, he knew not whose.
canbe10{_}115{_}Halpin Frayser was not much of a philosopher, nor a scientist.
canbe10{_}116{_}The circumstance that, waking from a deep sleep at night in the midst of a forest, he had spoken aloud a name that he had not in memory and hardly had in mind did not arouse an enlightened curiosity to investigate the phenomenon.
canbe10{_}117{_}He thought it odd, and with a little perfunctory shiver, as if in deference to a seasonal presumption that the night was chill, he lay down again and went to sleep.
canbe10{_}118{_}But his sleep was no longer dreamless.
canbe10{_}119{_}He thought he was walking along a dusty road that showed white in the gathering darkness of a summer night.
canbe10{_}120{_}Whence and whither it led, and why he traveled it, he did not know, though all seemed simple and natural, as is the way in dreams; for in the Land Beyond the Bed surprises cease from troubling and the judgment is at rest.
canbe10{_}121{_}Soon he came to a parting of the ways; leading from the highway was a road less traveled, having the appearance, indeed, of having been long abandoned, because, he thought, it led to something evil; yet he turned into it without hesitation, impelled by some imperious necessity.
canbe10{_}122{_}As he pressed forward he became conscious that his way was haunted by invisible existences whom he could not definitely figure to his mind.
canbe10{_}123{_}From among the trees on either side he caught broken and incoherent whispers in a strange tongue which yet he partly understood.
canbe10{_}124{_}They seemed to him fragmentary utterances of a monstrous conspiracy against his body and soul.
canbe10{_}125{_}It was now long after nightfall, yet the interminable forest through which he journeyed was lit with a wan glimmer having no point of diffusion, for in its mysterious lumination nothing cast a shadow.
canbe10{_}126{_}A shallow pool in the guttered depression of an old wheel rut, as from a recent rain, met his eye with a crimson gleam.
canbe10{_}127{_}He stooped and plunged his hand into it.
canbe10{_}128{_}It stained his fingers; it was blood!
canbe10{_}129{_}Blood, he then observed, was about him everywhere.
canbe10{_}130{_}The weeds growing rankly by the roadside showed it in blots and splashes on their big, broad leaves.
canbe10{_}131{_}Patches of dry dust between the wheelways were pitted and spattered as with a red rain.
canbe10{_}132{_}Defiling the trunks of the trees were broad maculations of crimson, and blood dripped like dew from their foliage.
canbe10{_}133{_}All this he observed with a terror which seemed not incompatible with the fulfillment of a natural expectation.
canbe10{_}134{_}It seemed to him that it was all in expiation of some crime which, though conscious of his guilt, he could not rightly remember.
canbe10{_}135{_}To the menaces and mysteries of his surroundings the consciousness was an added horror.
canbe10{_}136{_}Vainly he sought by tracing life backward in memory, to reproduce the moment of his sin; scenes and incidents came crowding tumultuously into his mind, one picture effacing another, or commingling with it in confusion and obscurity, but nowhere could he catch a glimpse of what he sought.
canbe10{_}137{_}The failure augmented his terror; he felt as one who has murdered in the dark, not knowing whom nor why.
canbe10{_}138{_}So frightful was the situation--the mysterious light burned with so silent and awful a menace; the noxious plants, the trees that by common consent are invested with a melancholy or baleful character, so openly in his sight conspired against his peace; from overhead and all about came so audible and startling whispers and the sighs of creatures so obviously not of earth--that he could endure it no longer, and with a great effort to break some malign spell that bound his faculties to silence and inaction, he shouted with the full strength of his lungs!
canbe10{_}139{_}His voice broken, it seemed, into an infinite multitude of unfamiliar sounds, went babbling and stammering away into the distant reaches of the forest, died into silence, and all was as before.
canbe10{_}140{_}But he had made a beginning at resistance and was encouraged.
canbe10{_}141{_}He said:  "I will not submit unheard.
canbe10{_}142{_}There may be powers that are not malignant traveling this accursed road.
canbe10{_}143{_}I shall leave them a record and an appeal.
canbe10{_}144{_}I shall relate my wrongs, the persecutions that I endure--I, a helpless mortal, a penitent, an unoffending poet!"
canbe10{_}145{_}Halpin Frayser was a poet only as he was a penitent:  in his dream.
canbe10{_}146{_}Taking from his clothing a small red-leather pocketbook, one-half of which was leaved for memoranda, he discovered that he was without a pencil.
canbe10{_}147{_}He broke a twig from a bush, dipped it into a pool of blood and wrote rapidly.
canbe10{_}148{_}He had hardly touched the paper with the point of his twig when a low, wild peal of laughter broke out at a measureless distance away, and growing ever louder, seemed approaching ever nearer; a soulless, heartless, and unjoyous laugh, like that of the loon, solitary by the lakeside at midnight; a laugh which culminated in an unearthly shout close at hand, then died away by slow gradations, as if the accursed being that uttered it had withdrawn over the verge of the world whence it had come.
canbe10{_}149{_}But the man felt that this was not so--that it was near by and had not moved.
canbe10{_}150{_}A strange sensation began slowly to take possession of his body and his mind.
canbe10{_}151{_}He could not have said which, if any, of his senses was affected; he felt it rather as a consciousness--a mysterious mental assurance of some overpowering presence--some supernatural malevolence different in kind from the invisible existences that swarmed about him, and superior to them in power.
canbe10{_}152{_}He knew that it had uttered that hideous laugh.
canbe10{_}153{_}And now it seemed to be approaching him; from what direction he did not know--dared not conjecture.
canbe10{_}154{_}All his former fears were forgotten or merged in the gigantic terror that now held him in thrall.
canbe10{_}155{_}Apart from that, he had but one thought:  to complete his written appeal to the benign powers who, traversing the haunted wood, might some time rescue him if he should be denied the blessing of annihilation.
canbe10{_}156{_}He wrote with terrible rapidity, the twig in his fingers rilling blood without renewal; but in the middle of a sentence his hands denied their service to his will, his arms fell to his sides, the book to the earth; and powerless to move or cry out, he found himself staring into the sharply drawn face and blank, dead eyes of his own mother, standing white and silent in the garments of the grave!
canbe10{_}157{_}II  In his youth Halpin Frayser had lived with his parents in Nashville, Tennessee.
canbe10{_}158{_}The Fraysers were well-to-do, having a good position in such society as had survived the wreck wrought by civil war.
canbe10{_}159{_}Their children had the social and educational opportunities of their time and place, and had responded to good associations and instruction with agreeable manners and cultivated minds.
canbe10{_}160{_}Halpin being the youngest and not over robust was perhaps a trifle "spoiled."
canbe10{_}161{_}He had the double disadvantage of a mother's assiduity and a father's neglect.
canbe10{_}162{_}Frayser pere was what no Southern man of means is not--a politician.
canbe10{_}163{_}His country, or rather his section and State, made demands upon his time and attention so exacting that to those of his family he was compelled to turn an ear partly deafened by the thunder of the political captains and the shouting, his own included.
canbe10{_}164{_}Young Halpin was of a dreamy, indolent and rather romantic turn, somewhat more addicted to literature than law, the profession to which he was bred.
canbe10{_}165{_}Among those of his relations who professed the modern faith of heredity it was well understood that in him the character of the late Myron Bayne, a maternal great-grandfather, had revisited the glimpses of the moon--by which orb Bayne had in his lifetime been sufficiently affected to be a poet of no small Colonial distinction.
canbe10{_}166{_}If not specially observed, it was observable that while a Frayser who was not the proud possessor of a sumptuous copy of the ancestral "poetical works" (printed at the family expense, and long ago withdrawn from an inhospitable market) was a rare Frayser indeed, there was an illogical indisposition to honor the great deceased in the person of his spiritual successor.
canbe10{_}167{_}Halpin was pretty generally deprecated as an intellectual black sheep who was likely at any moment to disgrace the flock by bleating in meter.
canbe10{_}168{_}The Tennessee Fraysers were a practical folk--not practical in the popular sense of devotion to sordid pursuits, but having a robust contempt for any qualities unfitting a man for the wholesome vocation of politics.
canbe10{_}169{_}In justice to young Halpin it should be said that while in him were pretty faithfully reproduced most of the mental and moral characteristics ascribed by history and family tradition to the famous Colonial bard, his succession to the gift and faculty divine was purely inferential.
canbe10{_}170{_}Not only had he never been known to court the muse, but in truth he could not have written correctly a line of verse to save himself from the Killer of the Wise.
canbe10{_}171{_}Still, there was no knowing when the dormant faculty might wake and smite the lyre.
canbe10{_}172{_}In the meantime the young man was rather a loose fish, anyhow.
canbe10{_}173{_}Between him and his mother was the most perfect sympathy, for secretly the lady was herself a devout disciple of the late and great Myron Bayne, though with the tact so generally and justly admired in her sex (despite the hardy calumniators who insist that it is essentially the same thing as cunning) she had always taken care to conceal her weakness from all eyes but those of him who shared it.
canbe10{_}174{_}Their common guilt in respect of that was an added tie between them.
canbe10{_}175{_}If in Halpin's youth his mother had "spoiled" him, he had assuredly done his part toward being spoiled.
canbe10{_}176{_}As he grew to such manhood as is attainable by a Southerner who does not care which way elections go the attachment between him and his beautiful mother--whom from early childhood he had called Katy--became yearly stronger and more tender.
canbe10{_}177{_}In these two romantic natures was manifest in a signal way that neglected phenomenon, the dominance of the sexual element in all the relations of life, strengthening, softening, and beautifying even those of consanguinity.
canbe10{_}178{_}The two were nearly inseparable, and by strangers observing their manner were not infrequently mistaken for lovers.
canbe10{_}179{_}Entering his mother's boudoir one day Halpin Frayser kissed her upon the forehead, toyed for a moment with a lock of her dark hair which had escaped from its confining pins, and said, with an obvious effort at calmness:  "Would you greatly mind, Katy, if I were called away to California for a few weeks?"
canbe10{_}180{_}It was hardly needful for Katy to answer with her lips a question to which her telltale cheeks had made instant reply.
canbe10{_}181{_}Evidently she would greatly mind; and the tears, too, sprang into her large brown eyes as corroborative testimony.
canbe10{_}182{_}"Ah, my son," she said, looking up into his face with infinite tenderness, "I should have known that this was coming.
canbe10{_}183{_}Did I not lie awake a half of the night weeping because, during the other half, Grandfather Bayne had come to me in a dream, and standing by his portrait--young, too, and handsome as that--pointed to yours on the same wall?
canbe10{_}184{_}And when I looked it seemed that I could not see the features; you had been painted with a face cloth, such as we put upon the dead.
canbe10{_}185{_}Your father has laughed at me, but you and I, dear, know that such things are not for nothing.
canbe10{_}186{_}And I saw below the edge of the cloth the marks of hands on your throat--forgive me, but we have not been used to keep such things from each other.
canbe10{_}187{_}Perhaps you have another interpretation.
canbe10{_}188{_}Perhaps it does not mean that you will go to California.
canbe10{_}189{_}Or maybe you will take me with you?"
canbe10{_}190{_}It must be confessed that this ingenious interpretation of the dream in the light of newly discovered evidence did not wholly commend itself to the son's more logical mind; he had, for the moment at least, a conviction that it foreshadowed a more simple and immediate, if less tragic, disaster than a visit to the Pacific Coast.
canbe10{_}191{_}It was Halpin Frayser's impression that he was to be garroted on his native heath.
canbe10{_}192{_}"Are there not medicinal springs in California?"
canbe10{_}193{_}Mrs. Frayser resumed before he had time to give her the true reading of the dream--"places where one recovers from rheumatism and neuralgia?
canbe10{_}194{_}Look--my fingers feel so stiff; and I am almost sure they have been giving me great pain while I slept."
canbe10{_}195{_}She held out her hands for his inspection.
canbe10{_}196{_}What diagnosis of her case the young man may have thought it best to conceal with a smile the historian is unable to state, but for himself he feels bound to say that fingers looking less stiff, and showing fewer evidences of even insensible pain, have seldom been submitted for medical inspection by even the fairest patient desiring a prescription of unfamiliar scenes.
canbe10{_}197{_}The outcome of it was that of these two odd persons having equally odd notions of duty, the one went to California, as the interest of his client required, and the other remained at home in compliance with a wish that her husband was scarcely conscious of entertaining.
canbe10{_}198{_}While in San Francisco Halpin Frayser was walking one dark night along the water front of the city, when, with a suddenness that surprised and disconcerted him, he became a sailor.
canbe10{_}199{_}He was in fact "shanghaied" aboard a gallant, gallant ship, and sailed for a far countree.
canbe10{_}200{_}Nor did his misfortunes end with the voyage; for the ship was cast ashore on an island of the South Pacific, and it was six years afterward when the survivors were taken off by a venturesome trading schooner and brought back to San Francisco.
canbe10{_}201{_}Though poor in purse, Frayser was no less proud in spirit than he had been in the years that seemed ages and ages ago.
canbe10{_}202{_}He would accept no assistance from strangers, and it was while living with a fellow survivor near the town of St. Helena, awaiting news and remittances from home, that he had gone gunning and dreaming.
canbe10{_}203{_}III  The apparition confronting the dreamer in the haunted wood--the thing so like, yet so unlike his mother--was horrible!
canbe10{_}204{_}It stirred no love nor longing in his heart; it came unattended with pleasant memories of a golden past--inspired no sentiment of any kind; all the finer emotions were swallowed up in fear.
canbe10{_}205{_}He tried to turn and run from before it, but his legs were as lead; he was unable to lift his feet from the ground.
canbe10{_}206{_}His arms hung helpless at his sides; of his eyes only he retained control, and these he dared not remove from the lusterless orbs of the apparition, which he knew was not a soul without a body, but that most dreadful of all existences infesting that haunted wood--a body without a soul!
canbe10{_}207{_}In its blank stare was neither love, nor pity, nor intelligence--nothing to which to address an appeal for mercy.
canbe10{_}208{_}"An appeal will not lie," he thought, with an absurd reversion to professional slang, making the situation more horrible, as the fire of a cigar might light up a tomb.
canbe10{_}209{_}For a time, which seemed so long that the world grew gray with age and sin, and the haunted forest, having fulfilled its purpose in this monstrous culmination of its terrors, vanished out of his consciousness with all its sights and sounds, the apparition stood within a pace, regarding him with the mindless malevolence of a wild brute; then thrust its hands forward and sprang upon him with appalling ferocity!
canbe10{_}210{_}The act released his physical energies without unfettering his will; his mind was still spellbound, but his powerful body and agile limbs, endowed with a blind, insensate life of their own, resisted stoutly and well.
canbe10{_}211{_}For an instant he seemed to see this unnatural contest between a dead intelligence and a breathing mechanism only as a spectator--such fancies are in dreams; then he regained his identity almost as if by a leap forward into his body, and the straining automaton had a directing will as alert and fierce as that of its hideous antagonist.
canbe10{_}212{_}But what mortal can cope with a creature of his dream?
canbe10{_}213{_}The imagination creating the enemy is already vanquished; the combat's result is the combat's cause.
canbe10{_}214{_}Despite his struggles--despite his strength and activity, which seemed wasted in a void, he felt the cold fingers close upon his throat.
canbe10{_}215{_}Borne backward to the earth, he saw above him the dead and drawn face within a hand's breadth of his own, and then all was black.
canbe10{_}216{_}A sound as of the beating of distant drums--a murmur of swarming voices, a sharp, far cry signing all to silence, and Halpin Frayser dreamed that he was dead.
canbe10{_}217{_}IV  A warm, clear night had been followed by a morning of drenching fog.
canbe10{_}218{_}At about the middle of the afternoon of the preceding day a little whiff of light vapor--a mere thickening of the atmosphere, the ghost of a cloud--had been observed clinging to the western side of Mount St. Helena, away up along the barren altitudes near the summit.
canbe10{_}219{_}It was so thin, so diaphanous, so like a fancy made visible, that one would have said:  "Look quickly!
canbe10{_}220{_}in a moment it will be gone."
canbe10{_}221{_}In a moment it was visibly larger and denser.
canbe10{_}222{_}While with one edge it clung to the mountain, with the other it reached farther and farther out into the air above the lower slopes.
canbe10{_}223{_}At the same time it extended itself to north and south, joining small patches of mist that appeared to come out of the mountainside on exactly the same level, with an intelligent design to be absorbed.
canbe10{_}224{_}And so it grew and grew until the summit was shut out of view from the valley, and over the valley itself was an ever-extending canopy, opaque and gray.
canbe10{_}225{_}At Calistoga, which lies near the head of the valley and the foot of the mountain, there were a starless night and a sunless morning.
canbe10{_}226{_}The fog, sinking into the valley, had reached southward, swallowing up ranch after ranch, until it had blotted out the town of St. Helena, nine miles away.
canbe10{_}227{_}The dust in the road was laid; trees were adrip with moisture; birds sat silent in their coverts; the morning light was wan and ghastly, with neither color nor fire.
canbe10{_}228{_}Two men left the town of St. Helena at the first glimmer of dawn, and walked along the road northward up the valley toward Calistoga.
canbe10{_}229{_}They carried guns on their shoulders, yet no one having knowledge of such matters could have mistaken them for hunters of bird or beast.
canbe10{_}230{_}They were a deputy sheriff from Napa and a detective from San Francisco-- Holker and Jaralson, respectively.
canbe10{_}231{_}Their business was man-hunting.
canbe10{_}232{_}"How far is it?"
canbe10{_}233{_}inquired Holker, as they strode along, their feet stirring white the dust beneath the damp surface of the road.
canbe10{_}234{_}"The White Church?
canbe10{_}235{_}Only a half mile farther," the other answered.
canbe10{_}236{_}"By the way," he added, "it is neither white nor a church; it is an abandoned schoolhouse, gray with age and neglect.
canbe10{_}237{_}Religious services were once held in it--when it was white, and there is a graveyard that would delight a poet.
canbe10{_}238{_}Can you guess why I sent for you, and told you to come heeled?"
canbe10{_}239{_}"Oh, I never have bothered you about things of that kind.
canbe10{_}240{_}I've always found you communicative when the time came.
canbe10{_}241{_}But if I may hazard a guess, you want me to help you arrest one of the corpses in the graveyard."
canbe10{_}242{_}"You remember Branscom?"
canbe10{_}243{_}said Jaralson, treating his companion's wit with the inattention that it deserved.
canbe10{_}244{_}"The chap who cut his wife's throat?
canbe10{_}245{_}I ought; I wasted a week's work on him and had my expenses for my trouble.
canbe10{_}246{_}There is a reward of five hundred dollars, but none of us ever got a sight of him.
canbe10{_}247{_}You don't mean to say--"  "Yes, I do.
canbe10{_}248{_}He has been under the noses of you fellows all the time.
canbe10{_}249{_}He comes by night to the old graveyard at the White Church."
canbe10{_}250{_}"The devil!
canbe10{_}251{_}That's where they buried his wife."
canbe10{_}252{_}"Well, you fellows might have had sense enough to suspect that he would return to her grave some time."
canbe10{_}253{_}"The very last place that anyone would have expected him to return to."
canbe10{_}254{_}"But you had exhausted all the other places.
canbe10{_}255{_}Learning your failure at them, I 'laid for him' there."
canbe10{_}256{_}"And you found him?"
canbe10{_}257{_}"Damn it!
canbe10{_}258{_}he found ME.
canbe10{_}259{_}The rascal got the drop on me--regularly held me up and made me travel.
canbe10{_}260{_}It's God's mercy that he didn't go through me.
canbe10{_}261{_}Oh, he's a good one, and I fancy the half of that reward is enough for me if you're needy."
canbe10{_}262{_}Holker laughed good humoredly, and explained that his creditors were never more importunate.
canbe10{_}263{_}"I wanted merely to show you the ground, and arrange a plan with you," the detective explained.
canbe10{_}264{_}"I thought it as well for us to be heeled, even in daylight."
canbe10{_}265{_}"The man must be insane," said the deputy sheriff.
canbe10{_}266{_}"The reward is for his capture and conviction.
canbe10{_}267{_}If he's mad he won't be convicted."
canbe10{_}268{_}Mr. Holker was so profoundly affected by that possible failure of justice that he involuntarily stopped in the middle of the road, then resumed his walk with abated zeal.
canbe10{_}269{_}"Well, he looks it," assented Jaralson.
canbe10{_}270{_}"I'm bound to admit that a more unshaven, unshorn, unkempt, and uneverything wretch I never saw outside the ancient and honorable order of tramps.
canbe10{_}271{_}But I've gone in for him, and can't make up my mind to let go.
canbe10{_}272{_}There's glory in it for us, anyhow.
canbe10{_}273{_}Not another soul knows that he is this side of the Mountains of the Moon."
canbe10{_}274{_}"All right," Holker said; "we will go and view the ground," and he added, in the words of a once favorite inscription for tombstones: "'where you must shortly lie'--I mean, if old Branscom ever gets tired of you and your impertinent intrusion.
canbe10{_}275{_}By the way, I heard the other day that 'Branscom' was not his real name."
canbe10{_}276{_}"What is?"
canbe10{_}277{_}"I can't recall it.
canbe10{_}278{_}I had lost all interest in the wretch, and it did not fix itself in my memory--something like Pardee.
canbe10{_}279{_}The woman whose throat he had the bad taste to cut was a widow when he met her.
canbe10{_}280{_}She had come to California to look up some relatives--there are persons who will do that sometimes.
canbe10{_}281{_}But you know all that."
canbe10{_}282{_}"Naturally."
canbe10{_}283{_}"But not knowing the right name, by what happy inspiration did you find the right grave?
canbe10{_}284{_}The man who told me what the name was said it had been cut on the headboard."
canbe10{_}285{_}"I don't know the right grave."
canbe10{_}286{_}Jaralson was apparently a trifle reluctant to admit his ignorance of so important a point of his plan.
canbe10{_}287{_}"I have been watching about the place generally.
canbe10{_}288{_}A part of our work this morning will be to identify that grave.
canbe10{_}289{_}Here is the White Church."
canbe10{_}290{_}For a long distance the road had been bordered by fields on both sides, but now on the left there was a forest of oaks, madronos, and gigantic spruces whose lower parts only could be seen, dim and ghostly in the fog.
canbe10{_}291{_}The undergrowth was, in places, thick, but nowhere impenetrable.
canbe10{_}292{_}For some moments Holker saw nothing of the building, but as they turned into the woods it revealed itself in faint gray outline through the fog, looking huge and far away.
canbe10{_}293{_}A few steps more, and it was within an arm's length, distinct, dark with moisture, and insignificant in size.
canbe10{_}294{_}It had the usual country- schoolhouse form--belonged to the packing-box order of architecture; had an underpinning of stones, a moss-grown roof, and blank window spaces, whence both glass and sash had long departed.
canbe10{_}295{_}It was ruined, but not a ruin--a typical Californian substitute for what are known to guide-bookers abroad as "monuments of the past."
canbe10{_}296{_}With scarcely a glance at this uninteresting structure Jaralson moved on into the dripping undergrowth beyond.
canbe10{_}297{_}"I will show you where he held me up," he said.
canbe10{_}298{_}"This is the graveyard."
canbe10{_}299{_}Here and there among the bushes were small inclosures containing graves, sometimes no more than one.
canbe10{_}300{_}They were recognized as graves by the discolored stones or rotting boards at head and foot, leaning at all angles, some prostrate; by the ruined picket fences surrounding them; or, infrequently, by the mound itself showing its gravel through the fallen leaves.
canbe10{_}301{_}In many instances nothing marked the spot where lay the vestiges of some poor mortal--who, leaving "a large circle of sorrowing friends," had been left by them in turn-- except a depression in the earth, more lasting than that in the spirits of the mourners.
canbe10{_}302{_}The paths, if any paths had been, were long obliterated; trees of a considerable size had been permitted to grow up from the graves and thrust aside with root or branch the inclosing fences.
canbe10{_}303{_}Over all was that air of abandonment and decay which seems nowhere so fit and significant as in a village of the forgotten dead.
canbe10{_}304{_}As the two men, Jaralson leading, pushed their way through the growth of young trees, that enterprising man suddenly stopped and brought up his shotgun to the height of his breast, uttered a low note of warning, and stood motionless, his eyes fixed upon something ahead.
canbe10{_}305{_}As well as he could, obstructed by brush, his companion, though seeing nothing, imitated the posture and so stood, prepared for what might ensue.
canbe10{_}306{_}A moment later Jaralson moved cautiously forward, the other following.
canbe10{_}307{_}Under the branches of an enormous spruce lay the dead body of a man.
canbe10{_}308{_}Standing silent above it they noted such particulars as first strike the attention--the face, the attitude, the clothing; whatever most promptly and plainly answers the unspoken question of a sympathetic curiosity.
canbe10{_}309{_}The body lay upon its back, the legs wide apart.
canbe10{_}310{_}One arm was thrust upward, the other outward; but the latter was bent acutely, and the hand was near the throat.
canbe10{_}311{_}Both hands were tightly clenched.
canbe10{_}312{_}The whole attitude was that of desperate but ineffectual resistance to-- what?
canbe10{_}313{_}Near by lay a shotgun and a game bag through the meshes of which was seen the plumage of shot birds.
canbe10{_}314{_}All about were evidences of a furious struggle; small sprouts of poison-oak were bent and denuded of leaf and bark; dead and rotting leaves had been pushed into heaps and ridges on both sides of the legs by the action of other feet than theirs; alongside the hips were unmistakable impressions of human knees.
canbe10{_}315{_}The nature of the struggle was made clear by a glance at the dead man's throat and face.
canbe10{_}316{_}While breast and hands were white, those were purple--almost black.
canbe10{_}317{_}The shoulders lay upon a low mound, and the head was turned back at an angle otherwise impossible, the expanded eyes staring blankly backward in a direction opposite to that of the feet.
canbe10{_}318{_}From the froth filling the open mouth the tongue protruded, black and swollen.
canbe10{_}319{_}The throat showed horrible contusions; not mere finger-marks, but bruises and lacerations wrought by two strong hands that must have buried themselves in the yielding flesh, maintaining their terrible grasp until long after death.
canbe10{_}320{_}Breast, throat, face, were wet; the clothing was saturated; drops of water, condensed from the fog, studded the hair and mustache.
canbe10{_}321{_}All this the two men observed without speaking--almost at a glance.
canbe10{_}322{_}Then Holker said:  "Poor devil!
canbe10{_}323{_}he had a rough deal."
canbe10{_}324{_}Jaralson was making a vigilant circumspection of the forest, his shotgun held in both hands and at full cock, his finger upon the trigger.
canbe10{_}325{_}"The work of a maniac," he said, without withdrawing his eyes from the inclosing wood.
canbe10{_}326{_}"It was done by Branscom--Pardee."
canbe10{_}327{_}Something half hidden by the disturbed leaves on the earth caught Holker's attention.
canbe10{_}328{_}It was a red-leather pocketbook.
canbe10{_}329{_}He picked it up and opened it.
canbe10{_}330{_}It contained leaves of white paper for memoranda, and upon the first leaf was the name "Halpin Frayser."
canbe10{_}331{_}Written in red on several succeeding leaves--scrawled as if in haste and barely legible--were the following lines, which Holker read aloud, while his companion continued scanning the dim gray confines of their narrow world and hearing matter of apprehension in the drip of water from every burdened branch:   "Enthralled by some mysterious spell, I stood In the lit gloom of an enchanted wood.
canbe10{_}332{_}The cypress there and myrtle twined their boughs, Significant, in baleful brotherhood.
canbe10{_}333{_}"The brooding willow whispered to the yew; Beneath, the deadly nightshade and the rue,    With immortelles self-woven into strange Funereal shapes, and horrid nettles grew.
canbe10{_}334{_}"No song of bird nor any drone of bees, Nor light leaf lifted by the wholesome breeze:    The air was stagnant all, and Silence was A living thing that breathed among the trees.
canbe10{_}335{_}"Conspiring spirits whispered in the gloom, Half-heard, the stilly secrets of the tomb.
canbe10{_}336{_}With blood the trees were all adrip; the leaves Shone in the witch-light with a ruddy bloom.
canbe10{_}337{_}"I cried aloud!--the spell, unbroken still, Rested upon my spirit and my will.
canbe10{_}338{_}Unsouled, unhearted, hopeless and forlorn, I strove with monstrous presages of ill!
canbe10{_}339{_}"At last the viewless--"   Holker ceased reading; there was no more to read.
canbe10{_}340{_}The manuscript broke off in the middle of a line.
canbe10{_}341{_}"That sounds like Bayne," said Jaralson, who was something of a scholar in his way.
canbe10{_}342{_}He had abated his vigilance and stood looking down at the body.
canbe10{_}343{_}"Who's Bayne?"
canbe10{_}344{_}Holker asked rather incuriously.
canbe10{_}345{_}"Myron Bayne, a chap who flourished in the early years of the nation- -more than a century ago.
canbe10{_}346{_}Wrote mighty dismal stuff; I have his collected works.
canbe10{_}347{_}That poem is not among them, but it must have been omitted by mistake."
canbe10{_}348{_}"It is cold," said Holker; "let us leave here; we must have up the coroner from Napa."
canbe10{_}349{_}Jaralson said nothing, but made a movement in compliance.
canbe10{_}350{_}Passing the end of the slight elevation of earth upon which the dead man's head and shoulders lay, his foot struck some hard substance under the rotting forest leaves, and he took the trouble to kick it into view.
canbe10{_}351{_}It was a fallen headboard, and painted on it were the hardly decipherable words, "Catharine Larue."
canbe10{_}352{_}"Larue, Larue!"
canbe10{_}353{_}exclaimed Holker, with sudden animation.
canbe10{_}354{_}"Why, that is the real name of Branscom--not Pardee.
canbe10{_}355{_}And--bless my soul!
canbe10{_}356{_}how it all comes to me--the murdered woman's name had been Frayser!"
canbe10{_}357{_}"There is some rascally mystery here," said Detective Jaralson.
canbe10{_}358{_}"I hate anything of that kind."
canbe10{_}359{_}There came to them out of the fog--seemingly from a great distance-- the sound of a laugh, a low, deliberate, soulless laugh, which had no more of joy than that of a hyena night-prowling in the desert; a laugh that rose by slow gradation, louder and louder, clearer, more distinct and terrible, until it seemed barely outside the narrow circle of their vision; a laugh so unnatural, so unhuman, so devilish, that it filled those hardy man-hunters with a sense of dread unspeakable!
canbe10{_}360{_}They did not move their weapons nor think of them; the menace of that horrible sound was not of the kind to be met with arms.
canbe10{_}361{_}As it had grown out of silence, so now it died away; from a culminating shout which had seemed almost in their ears, it drew itself away into the distance, until its failing notes, joyless and mechanical to the last, sank to silence at a measureless remove.
canbe10{_}362{_}THE SECRET OF MACARGER'S GULCH    North Westwardly from Indian Hill, about nine miles as the crow flies, is Macarger's Gulch.
canbe10{_}363{_}It is not much of a gulch--a mere depression between two wooded ridges of inconsiderable height.
canbe10{_}364{_}From its mouth up to its head--for gulches, like rivers, have an anatomy of their own--the distance does not exceed two miles, and the width at bottom is at only one place more than a dozen yards; for most of the distance on either side of the little brook which drains it in winter, and goes dry in the early spring, there is no level ground at all; the steep slopes of the hills, covered with an almost impenetrable growth of manzanita and chemisal, are parted by nothing but the width of the water course.
canbe10{_}365{_}No one but an occasional enterprising hunter of the vicinity ever goes into Macarger's Gulch, and five miles away it is unknown, even by name.
canbe10{_}366{_}Within that distance in any direction are far more conspicuous topographical features without names, and one might try in vain to ascertain by local inquiry the origin of the name of this one.
canbe10{_}367{_}About midway between the head and the mouth of Macarger's Gulch, the hill on the right as you ascend is cloven by another gulch, a short dry one, and at the junction of the two is a level space of two or three acres, and there a few years ago stood an old board house containing one small room.
canbe10{_}368{_}How the component parts of the house, few and simple as they were, had been assembled at that almost inaccessible point is a problem in the solution of which there would be greater satisfaction than advantage.
canbe10{_}369{_}Possibly the creek bed is a reformed road.
canbe10{_}370{_}It is certain that the gulch was at one time pretty thoroughly prospected by miners, who must have had some means of getting in with at least pack animals carrying tools and supplies; their profits, apparently, were not such as would have justified any considerable outlay to connect Macarger's Gulch with any center of civilization enjoying the distinction of a sawmill.
canbe10{_}371{_}The house, however, was there, most of it.
canbe10{_}372{_}It lacked a door and a window frame, and the chimney of mud and stones had fallen into an unlovely heap, overgrown with rank weeds.
canbe10{_}373{_}Such humble furniture as there may once have been and much of the lower weatherboarding, had served as fuel in the camp fires of hunters; as had also, probably, the curbing of an old well, which at the time I write of existed in the form of a rather wide but not very deep depression near by.
canbe10{_}374{_}One afternoon in the summer of 1874, I passed up Macarger's Gulch from the narrow valley into which it opens, by following the dry bed of the brook.
canbe10{_}375{_}I was quail-shooting and had made a bag of about a dozen birds by the time I had reached the house described, of whose existence I was until then unaware.
canbe10{_}376{_}After rather carelessly inspecting the ruin I resumed my sport, and having fairly good success prolonged it until near sunset, when it occurred to me that I was a long way from any human habitation--too far to reach one by nightfall.
canbe10{_}377{_}But in my game bag was food, and the old house would afford shelter, if shelter were needed on a warm and dewless night in the foothills of the Sierra Nevada, where one may sleep in comfort on the pine needles, without covering.
canbe10{_}378{_}I am fond of solitude and love the night, so my resolution to "camp out" was soon taken, and by the time that it was dark I had made my bed of boughs and grasses in a corner of the room and was roasting a quail at a fire that I had kindled on the hearth.
canbe10{_}379{_}The smoke escaped out of the ruined chimney, the light illuminated the room with a kindly glow, and as I ate my simple meal of plain bird and drank the remains of a bottle of red wine which had served me all the afternoon in place of the water, which the region did not supply, I experienced a sense of comfort which better fare and accommodations do not always give.
canbe10{_}380{_}Nevertheless, there was something lacking.
canbe10{_}381{_}I had a sense of comfort, but not of security.
canbe10{_}382{_}I detected myself staring more frequently at the open doorway and blank window than I could find warrant for doing.
canbe10{_}383{_}Outside these apertures all was black, and I was unable to repress a certain feeling of apprehension as my fancy pictured the outer world and filled it with unfriendly entities, natural and supernatural--chief among which, in their respective classes, were the grizzly bear, which I knew was occasionally still seen in that region, and the ghost, which I had reason to think was not.
canbe10{_}384{_}Unfortunately, our feelings do not always respect the law of probabilities, and to me that evening, the possible and the impossible were equally disquieting.
canbe10{_}385{_}Everyone who has had experience in the matter must have observed that one confronts the actual and imaginary perils of the night with far less apprehension in the open air than in a house with an open doorway.
canbe10{_}386{_}I felt this now as I lay on my leafy couch in a corner of the room next to the chimney and permitted my fire to die out.
canbe10{_}387{_}So strong became my sense of the presence of something malign and menacing in the place, that I found myself almost unable to withdraw my eyes from the opening, as in the deepening darkness it became more and more indistinct.
canbe10{_}388{_}And when the last little flame flickered and went out I grasped the shotgun which I had laid at my side and actually turned the muzzle in the direction of the now invisible entrance, my thumb on one of the hammers, ready to cock the piece, my breath suspended, my muscles rigid and tense.
canbe10{_}389{_}But later I laid down the weapon with a sense of shame and mortification.
canbe10{_}390{_}What did I fear, and why?--I, to whom the night had been      a more familiar face Than that of man -   I, in whom that element of hereditary superstition from which none of us is altogether free had given to solitude and darkness and silence only a more alluring interest and charm!
canbe10{_}391{_}I was unable to comprehend my folly, and losing in the conjecture the thing conjectured of, I fell asleep.
canbe10{_}392{_}And then I dreamed.
canbe10{_}393{_}I was in a great city in a foreign land--a city whose people were of my own race, with minor differences of speech and costume; yet precisely what these were I could not say; my sense of them was indistinct.
canbe10{_}394{_}The city was dominated by a great castle upon an overlooking height whose name I knew, but could not speak.
canbe10{_}395{_}I walked through many streets, some broad and straight with high, modern buildings, some narrow, gloomy, and tortuous, between the gables of quaint old houses whose overhanging stories, elaborately ornamented with carvings in wood and stone, almost met above my head.
canbe10{_}396{_}I sought someone whom I had never seen, yet knew that I should recognize when found.
canbe10{_}397{_}My quest was not aimless and fortuitous; it had a definite method.
canbe10{_}398{_}I turned from one street into another without hesitation and threaded a maze of intricate passages, devoid of the fear of losing my way.
canbe10{_}399{_}Presently I stopped before a low door in a plain stone house which might have been the dwelling of an artisan of the better sort, and without announcing myself, entered.
canbe10{_}400{_}The room, rather sparely furnished, and lighted by a single window with small diamond-shaped panes, had but two occupants; a man and a woman.
canbe10{_}401{_}They took no notice of my intrusion, a circumstance which, in the manner of dreams, appeared entirely natural.
canbe10{_}402{_}They were not conversing; they sat apart, unoccupied and sullen.
canbe10{_}403{_}The woman was young and rather stout, with fine large eyes and a certain grave beauty; my memory of her expression is exceedingly vivid, but in dreams one does not observe the details of faces.
canbe10{_}404{_}About her shoulders was a plaid shawl.
canbe10{_}405{_}The man was older, dark, with an evil face made more forbidding by a long scar extending from near the left temple diagonally downward into the black mustache; though in my dreams it seemed rather to haunt the face as a thing apart--I can express it no otherwise--than to belong to it.
canbe10{_}406{_}The moment that I found the man and woman I knew them to be husband and wife.
canbe10{_}407{_}What followed, I remember indistinctly; all was confused and inconsistent--made so, I think, by gleams of consciousness.
canbe10{_}408{_}It was as if two pictures, the scene of my dream, and my actual surroundings, had been blended, one overlying the other, until the former, gradually fading, disappeared, and I was broad awake in the deserted cabin, entirely and tranquilly conscious of my situation.
canbe10{_}409{_}My foolish fear was gone, and opening my eyes I saw that my fire, not altogether burned out, had revived by the falling of a stick and was again lighting the room.
canbe10{_}410{_}I had probably slept only a few minutes, but my commonplace dream had somehow so strongly impressed me that I was no longer drowsy; and after a little while I rose, pushed the embers of my fire together, and lighting my pipe proceeded in a rather ludicrously methodical way to meditate upon my vision.
canbe10{_}411{_}It would have puzzled me then to say in what respect it was worth attention.
canbe10{_}412{_}In the first moment of serious thought that I gave to the matter I recognized the city of my dream as Edinburgh, where I had never been; so if the dream was a memory it was a memory of pictures and description.
canbe10{_}413{_}The recognition somehow deeply impressed me; it was as if something in my mind insisted rebelliously against will and reason on the importance of all this.
canbe10{_}414{_}And that faculty, whatever it was, asserted also a control of my speech.
canbe10{_}415{_}"Surely," I said aloud, quite involuntarily, "the MacGregors must have come here from Edinburgh."
canbe10{_}416{_}At the moment, neither the substance of this remark nor the fact of my making it, surprised me in the least; it seemed entirely natural that I should know the name of my dreamfolk and something of their history.
canbe10{_}417{_}But the absurdity of it all soon dawned upon me:  I laughed aloud, knocked the ashes from my pipe and again stretched myself upon my bed of boughs and grass, where I lay staring absently into my failing fire, with no further thought of either my dream or my surroundings.
canbe10{_}418{_}Suddenly the single remaining flame crouched for a moment, then, springing upward, lifted itself clear of its embers and expired in air.
canbe10{_}419{_}The darkness was absolute.
canbe10{_}420{_}At that instant--almost, it seemed, before the gleam of the blaze had faded from my eyes--there was a dull, dead sound, as of some heavy body falling upon the floor, which shook beneath me as I lay.
canbe10{_}421{_}I sprang to a sitting posture and groped at my side for my gun; my notion was that some wild beast had leaped in through the open window.
canbe10{_}422{_}While the flimsy structure was still shaking from the impact I heard the sound of blows, the scuffling of feet upon the floor, and then--it seemed to come from almost within reach of my hand, the sharp shrieking of a woman in mortal agony.
canbe10{_}423{_}So horrible a cry I had never heard nor conceived; it utterly unnerved me; I was conscious for a moment of nothing but my own terror!
canbe10{_}424{_}Fortunately my hand now found the weapon of which it was in search, and the familiar touch somewhat restored me.
canbe10{_}425{_}I leaped to my feet, straining my eyes to pierce the darkness.
canbe10{_}426{_}The violent sounds had ceased, but more terrible than these, I heard, at what seemed long intervals, the faint intermittent gasping of some living, dying thing!
canbe10{_}427{_}As my eyes grew accustomed to the dim light of the coals in the fireplace, I saw first the shapes of the door and window, looking blacker than the black of the walls.
canbe10{_}428{_}Next, the distinction between wall and floor became discernible, and at last I was sensible to the form and full expanse of the floor from end to end and side to side.
canbe10{_}429{_}Nothing was visible and the silence was unbroken.
canbe10{_}430{_}With a hand that shook a little, the other still grasping my gun, I restored my fire and made a critical examination of the place.
canbe10{_}431{_}There was nowhere any sign that the cabin had been entered.
canbe10{_}432{_}My own tracks were visible in the dust covering the floor, but there were no others.
canbe10{_}433{_}I relit my pipe, provided fresh fuel by ripping a thin board or two from the inside of the house--I did not care to go into the darkness out of doors--and passed the rest of the night smoking and thinking, and feeding my fire; not for added years of life would I have permitted that little flame to expire again.
canbe10{_}434{_}Some years afterward I met in Sacramento a man named Morgan, to whom I had a note of introduction from a friend in San Francisco.
canbe10{_}435{_}Dining with him one evening at his home I observed various "trophies" upon the wall, indicating that he was fond of shooting.
canbe10{_}436{_}It turned out that he was, and in relating some of his feats he mentioned having been in the region of my adventure.
canbe10{_}437{_}"Mr. Morgan," I asked abruptly, "do you know a place up there called Macarger's Gulch?"
canbe10{_}438{_}"I have good reason to," he replied; "it was I who gave to the newspapers, last year, the accounts of the finding of the skeleton there."
canbe10{_}439{_}I had not heard of it; the accounts had been published, it appeared, while I was absent in the East.
canbe10{_}440{_}"By the way," said Morgan, "the name of the gulch is a corruption; it should have been called 'MacGregor's.'
canbe10{_}441{_}My dear," he added, speaking to his wife, "Mr. Elderson has upset his wine."
canbe10{_}442{_}That was hardly accurate--I had simply dropped it, glass and all.
canbe10{_}443{_}"There was an old shanty once in the gulch," Morgan resumed when the ruin wrought by my awkwardness had been repaired, "but just previously to my visit it had been blown down, or rather blown away, for its debris was scattered all about, the very floor being parted, plank from plank.
canbe10{_}444{_}Between two of the sleepers still in position I and my companion observed the remnant of a plaid shawl, and examining it found that it was wrapped about the shoulders of the body of a woman, of which but little remained besides the bones, partly covered with fragments of clothing, and brown dry skin.
canbe10{_}445{_}But we will spare Mrs. Morgan," he added with a smile.
canbe10{_}446{_}The lady had indeed exhibited signs of disgust rather than sympathy.
canbe10{_}447{_}"It is necessary to say, however," he went on, "that the skull was fractured in several places, as by blows of some blunt instrument; and that instrument itself--a pick-handle, still stained with blood-- lay under the boards near by."
canbe10{_}448{_}Mr. Morgan turned to his wife.
canbe10{_}449{_}"Pardon me, my dear," he said with affected solemnity, "for mentioning these disagreeable particulars, the natural though regrettable incidents of a conjugal quarrel-- resulting, doubtless, from the luckless wife's insubordination."
canbe10{_}450{_}"I ought to be able to overlook it," the lady replied with composure; "you have so many times asked me to in those very words."
canbe10{_}451{_}I thought he seemed rather glad to go on with his story.
canbe10{_}452{_}"From these and other circumstances," he said, "the coroner's jury found that the deceased, Janet MacGregor, came to her death from blows inflicted by some person to the jury unknown; but it was added that the evidence pointed strongly to her husband, Thomas MacGregor, as the guilty person.
canbe10{_}453{_}But Thomas MacGregor has never been found nor heard of.
canbe10{_}454{_}It was learned that the couple came from Edinburgh, but not--my dear, do you not observe that Mr. Elderson's boneplate has water in it?"
canbe10{_}455{_}I had deposited a chicken bone in my finger bowl.
canbe10{_}456{_}"In a little cupboard I found a photograph of MacGregor, but it did not lead to his capture."
canbe10{_}457{_}"Will you let me see it?"
canbe10{_}458{_}I said.
canbe10{_}459{_}The picture showed a dark man with an evil face made more forbidding by a long scar extending from near the temple diagonally downward into the black mustache.
canbe10{_}460{_}"By the way, Mr. Elderson," said my affable host, "may I know why you asked about 'Macarger's Gulch'?"
canbe10{_}461{_}"I lost a mule near there once," I replied, "and the mischance has-- has quite--upset me."
canbe10{_}462{_}"My dear," said Mr. Morgan, with the mechanical intonation of an interpreter translating, "the loss of Mr. Elderson's mule has peppered his coffee."
canbe10{_}463{_}ONE SUMMER NIGHT    The fact that Henry Armstrong was buried did not seem to him to prove that he was dead:  he had always been a hard man to convince.
canbe10{_}464{_}That he really was buried, the testimony of his senses compelled him to admit.
canbe10{_}465{_}His posture--flat upon his back, with his hands crossed upon his stomach and tied with something that he easily broke without profitably altering the situation--the strict confinement of his entire person, the black darkness and profound silence, made a body of evidence impossible to controvert and he accepted it without cavil.
canbe10{_}466{_}But dead--no; he was only very, very ill.
canbe10{_}467{_}He had, withal, the invalid's apathy and did not greatly concern himself about the uncommon fate that had been allotted to him.
canbe10{_}468{_}No philosopher was he-- just a plain, commonplace person gifted, for the time being, with a pathological indifference:  the organ that he feared consequences with was torpid.
canbe10{_}469{_}So, with no particular apprehension for his immediate future, he fell asleep and all was peace with Henry Armstrong.
canbe10{_}470{_}But something was going on overhead.
canbe10{_}471{_}It was a dark summer night, shot through with infrequent shimmers of lightning silently firing a cloud lying low in the west and portending a storm.
canbe10{_}472{_}These brief, stammering illuminations brought out with ghastly distinctness the monuments and headstones of the cemetery and seemed to set them dancing.
canbe10{_}473{_}It was not a night in which any credible witness was likely to be straying about a cemetery, so the three men who were there, digging into the grave of Henry Armstrong, felt reasonably secure.
canbe10{_}474{_}Two of them were young students from a medical college a few miles away; the third was a gigantic negro known as Jess.
canbe10{_}475{_}For many years Jess had been employed about the cemetery as a man-of-all-work and it was his favorite pleasantry that he knew "every soul in the place."
canbe10{_}476{_}From the nature of what he was now doing it was inferable that the place was not so populous as its register may have shown it to be.
canbe10{_}477{_}Outside the wall, at the part of the grounds farthest from the public road, were a horse and a light wagon, waiting.
canbe10{_}478{_}The work of excavation was not difficult:  the earth with which the grave had been loosely filled a few hours before offered little resistance and was soon thrown out.
canbe10{_}479{_}Removal of the casket from its box was less easy, but it was taken out, for it was a perquisite of Jess, who carefully unscrewed the cover and laid it aside, exposing the body in black trousers and white shirt.
canbe10{_}480{_}At that instant the air sprang to flame, a cracking shock of thunder shook the stunned world and Henry Armstrong tranquilly sat up.
canbe10{_}481{_}With inarticulate cries the men fled in terror, each in a different direction.
canbe10{_}482{_}For nothing on earth could two of them have been persuaded to return.
canbe10{_}483{_}But Jess was of another breed.
canbe10{_}484{_}In the gray of the morning the two students, pallid and haggard from anxiety and with the terror of their adventure still beating tumultuously in their blood, met at the medical college.
canbe10{_}485{_}"You saw it?"
canbe10{_}486{_}cried one.
canbe10{_}487{_}"God!
canbe10{_}488{_}yes--what are we to do?"
canbe10{_}489{_}They went around to the rear of the building, where they saw a horse, attached to a light wagon, hitched to a gatepost near the door of the dissecting-room.
canbe10{_}490{_}Mechanically they entered the room.
canbe10{_}491{_}On a bench in the obscurity sat the negro Jess.
canbe10{_}492{_}He rose, grinning, all eyes and teeth.
canbe10{_}493{_}"I'm waiting for my pay," he said.
canbe10{_}494{_}Stretched naked on a long table lay the body of Henry Armstrong, the head defiled with blood and clay from a blow with a spade.
canbe10{_}495{_}THE MOONLIT ROAD    I--STATEMENT OF JOEL HETMAN, JR.
canbe10{_}496{_}I am the most unfortunate of men.
canbe10{_}497{_}Rich, respected, fairly well educated and of sound health--with many other advantages usually valued by those having them and coveted by those who have them not--I sometimes think that I should be less unhappy if they had been denied me, for then the contrast between my outer and my inner life would not be continually demanding a painful attention.
canbe10{_}498{_}In the stress of privation and the need of effort I might sometimes forget the somber secret ever baffling the conjecture that it compels.
canbe10{_}499{_}I am the only child of Joel and Julia Hetman.
canbe10{_}500{_}The one was a well-to- do country gentleman, the other a beautiful and accomplished woman to whom he was passionately attached with what I now know to have been a jealous and exacting devotion.
canbe10{_}501{_}The family home was a few miles from Nashville, Tennessee, a large, irregularly built dwelling of no particular order of architecture, a little way off the road, in a park of trees and shrubbery.
canbe10{_}502{_}At the time of which I write I was nineteen years old, a student at Yale.
canbe10{_}503{_}One day I received a telegram from my father of such urgency that in compliance with its unexplained demand I left at once for home.
canbe10{_}504{_}At the railway station in Nashville a distant relative awaited me to apprise me of the reason for my recall:  my mother had been barbarously murdered--why and by whom none could conjecture, but the circumstances were these:  My father had gone to Nashville, intending to return the next afternoon.
canbe10{_}505{_}Something prevented his accomplishing the business in hand, so he returned on the same night, arriving just before the dawn.
canbe10{_}506{_}In his testimony before the coroner he explained that having no latchkey and not caring to disturb the sleeping servants, he had, with no clearly defined intention, gone round to the rear of the house.
canbe10{_}507{_}As he turned an angle of the building, he heard a sound as of a door gently closed, and saw in the darkness, indistinctly, the figure of a man, which instantly disappeared among the trees of the lawn.
canbe10{_}508{_}A hasty pursuit and brief search of the grounds in the belief that the trespasser was some one secretly visiting a servant proving fruitless, he entered at the unlocked door and mounted the stairs to my mother's chamber.
canbe10{_}509{_}Its door was open, and stepping into black darkness he fell headlong over some heavy object on the floor.
canbe10{_}510{_}I may spare myself the details; it was my poor mother, dead of strangulation by human hands!
canbe10{_}511{_}Nothing had been taken from the house, the servants had heard no sound, and excepting those terrible finger-marks upon the dead woman's throat--dear God!
canbe10{_}512{_}that I might forget them!--no trace of the assassin was ever found.
canbe10{_}513{_}I gave up my studies and remained with my father, who, naturally, was greatly changed.
canbe10{_}514{_}Always of a sedate, taciturn disposition, he now fell into so deep a dejection that nothing could hold his attention, yet anything--a footfall, the sudden closing of a door--aroused in him a fitful interest; one might have called it an apprehension.
canbe10{_}515{_}At any small surprise of the senses he would start visibly and sometimes turn pale, then relapse into a melancholy apathy deeper than before.
canbe10{_}516{_}I suppose he was what is called a "nervous wreck."
canbe10{_}517{_}As to me, I was younger then than now--there is much in that.
canbe10{_}518{_}Youth is Gilead, in which is balm for every wound.
canbe10{_}519{_}Ah, that I might again dwell in that enchanted land!
canbe10{_}520{_}Unacquainted with grief, I knew not how to appraise my bereavement; I could not rightly estimate the strength of the stroke.
canbe10{_}521{_}One night, a few months after the dreadful event, my father and I walked home from the city.
canbe10{_}522{_}The full moon was about three hours above the eastern horizon; the entire countryside had the solemn stillness of a summer night; our footfalls and the ceaseless song of the katydids were the only sound aloof.
canbe10{_}523{_}Black shadows of bordering trees lay athwart the road, which, in the short reaches between, gleamed a ghostly white.
canbe10{_}524{_}As we approached the gate to our dwelling, whose front was in shadow, and in which no light shone, my father suddenly stopped and clutched my arm, saying, hardly above his breath:  "God!
canbe10{_}525{_}God!
canbe10{_}526{_}what is that?"
canbe10{_}527{_}"I hear nothing," I replied.
canbe10{_}528{_}"But see--see!"
canbe10{_}529{_}he said, pointing along the road, directly ahead.
canbe10{_}530{_}I said:  "Nothing is there.
canbe10{_}531{_}Come, father, let us go in--you are ill."  He had released my arm and was standing rigid and motionless in the center of the illuminated roadway, staring like one bereft of sense.
canbe10{_}532{_}His face in the moonlight showed a pallor and fixity inexpressibly distressing.
canbe10{_}533{_}I pulled gently at his sleeve, but he had forgotten my existence.
canbe10{_}534{_}Presently he began to retire backward, step by step, never for an instant removing his eyes from what he saw, or thought he saw.
canbe10{_}535{_}I turned half round to follow, but stood irresolute.
canbe10{_}536{_}I do not recall any feeling of fear, unless a sudden chill was its physical manifestation.
canbe10{_}537{_}It seemed as if an icy wind had touched my face and enfolded my body from head to foot; I could feel the stir of it in my hair.
canbe10{_}538{_}At that moment my attention was drawn to a light that suddenly streamed from an upper window of the house:  one of the servants, awakened by what mysterious premonition of evil who can say, and in obedience to an impulse that she was never able to name, had lit a lamp.
canbe10{_}539{_}When I turned to look for my father he was gone, and in all the years that have passed no whisper of his fate has come across the borderland of conjecture from the realm of the unknown.
canbe10{_}540{_}II--STATEMENT OF CASPAR GRATTAN  To-day I am said to live; to-morrow, here in this room, will lie a senseless shape of clay that all too long was I.
canbe10{_}541{_}If anyone lift the cloth from the face of that unpleasant thing it will be in gratification of a mere morbid curiosity.
canbe10{_}542{_}Some, doubtless, will go further and inquire, "Who was he?"
canbe10{_}543{_}In this writing I supply the only answer that I am able to make--Caspar Grattan.
canbe10{_}544{_}Surely, that should be enough.
canbe10{_}545{_}The name has served my small need for more than twenty years of a life of unknown length.
canbe10{_}546{_}True, I gave it to myself, but lacking another I had the right.
canbe10{_}547{_}In this world one must have a name; it prevents confusion, even when it does not establish identity.
canbe10{_}548{_}Some, though, are known by numbers, which also seem inadequate distinctions.
canbe10{_}549{_}One day, for illustration, I was passing along a street of a city, far from here, when I met two men in uniform, one of whom, half pausing and looking curiously into my face, said to his companion, "That man looks like 767."
canbe10{_}550{_}Something in the number seemed familiar and horrible.
canbe10{_}551{_}Moved by an uncontrollable impulse, I sprang into a side street and ran until I fell exhausted in a country lane.
canbe10{_}552{_}I have never forgotten that number, and always it comes to memory attended by gibbering obscenity, peals of joyless laughter, the clang of iron doors.
canbe10{_}553{_}So I say a name, even if self-bestowed, is better than a number.
canbe10{_}554{_}In the register of the potter's field I shall soon have both.
canbe10{_}555{_}What wealth!
canbe10{_}556{_}Of him who shall find this paper I must beg a little consideration.
canbe10{_}557{_}It is not the history of my life; the knowledge to write that is denied me.
canbe10{_}558{_}This is only a record of broken and apparently unrelated memories, some of them as distinct and sequent as brilliant beads upon a thread, others remote and strange, having the character of crimson dreams with interspaces blank and black--witch-fires glowing still and red in a great desolation.
canbe10{_}559{_}Standing upon the shore of eternity, I turn for a last look landward over the course by which I came.
canbe10{_}560{_}There are twenty years of footprints fairly distinct, the impressions of bleeding feet.
canbe10{_}561{_}They lead through poverty and pain, devious and unsure, as of one staggering beneath a burden -   Remote, unfriended, melancholy, slow.
canbe10{_}562{_}Ah, the poet's prophecy of Me--how admirable, how dreadfully admirable!
canbe10{_}563{_}Backward beyond the beginning of this via dolorosa--this epic of suffering with episodes of sin--I see nothing clearly; it comes out of a cloud.
canbe10{_}564{_}I know that it spans only twenty years, yet I am an old man.
canbe10{_}565{_}One does not remember one's birth--one has to be told.
canbe10{_}566{_}But with me it was different; life came to me full-handed and dowered me with all my faculties and powers.
canbe10{_}567{_}Of a previous existence I know no more than others, for all have stammering intimations that may be memories and may be dreams.
canbe10{_}568{_}I know only that my first consciousness was of maturity in body and mind--a consciousness accepted without surprise or conjecture.
canbe10{_}569{_}I merely found myself walking in a forest, half-clad, footsore, unutterably weary and hungry.
canbe10{_}570{_}Seeing a farmhouse, I approached and asked for food, which was given me by one who inquired my name.
canbe10{_}571{_}I did not know, yet knew that all had names.
canbe10{_}572{_}Greatly embarrassed, I retreated, and night coming on, lay down in the forest and slept.
canbe10{_}573{_}The next day I entered a large town which I shall not name.
canbe10{_}574{_}Nor shall I recount further incidents of the life that is now to end--a life of wandering, always and everywhere haunted by an overmastering sense of crime in punishment of wrong and of terror in punishment of crime.
canbe10{_}575{_}Let me see if I can reduce it to narrative.
canbe10{_}576{_}I seem once to have lived near a great city, a prosperous planter, married to a woman whom I loved and distrusted.
canbe10{_}577{_}We had, it sometimes seems, one child, a youth of brilliant parts and promise.
canbe10{_}578{_}He is at all times a vague figure, never clearly drawn, frequently altogether out of the picture.
canbe10{_}579{_}One luckless evening it occurred to me to test my wife's fidelity in a vulgar, commonplace way familiar to everyone who has acquaintance with the literature of fact and fiction.
canbe10{_}580{_}I went to the city, telling my wife that I should be absent until the following afternoon.
canbe10{_}581{_}But I returned before daybreak and went to the rear of the house, purposing to enter by a door with which I had secretly so tampered that it would seem to lock, yet not actually fasten.
canbe10{_}582{_}As I approached it, I heard it gently open and close, and saw a man steal away into the darkness.
canbe10{_}583{_}With murder in my heart, I sprang after him, but he had vanished without even the bad luck of identification.
canbe10{_}584{_}Sometimes now I cannot even persuade myself that it was a human being.
canbe10{_}585{_}Crazed with jealousy and rage, blind and bestial with all the elemental passions of insulted manhood, I entered the house and sprang up the stairs to the door of my wife's chamber.
canbe10{_}586{_}It was closed, but having tampered with its lock also, I easily entered and despite the black darkness soon stood by the side of her bed.
canbe10{_}587{_}My groping hands told me that although disarranged it was unoccupied.
canbe10{_}588{_}"She is below," I thought, "and terrified by my entrance has evaded me in the darkness of the hall."
canbe10{_}589{_}With the purpose of seeking her I turned to leave the room, but took a wrong direction--the right one!
canbe10{_}590{_}My foot struck her, cowering in a corner of the room.
canbe10{_}591{_}Instantly my hands were at her throat, stifling a shriek, my knees were upon her struggling body; and there in the darkness, without a word of accusation or reproach, I strangled her till she died!
canbe10{_}592{_}There ends the dream.
canbe10{_}593{_}I have related it in the past tense, but the present would be the fitter form, for again and again the somber tragedy reenacts itself in my consciousness--over and over I lay the plan, I suffer the confirmation, I redress the wrong.
canbe10{_}594{_}Then all is blank; and afterward the rains beat against the grimy window-panes, or the snows fall upon my scant attire, the wheels rattle in the squalid streets where my life lies in poverty and mean employment.
canbe10{_}595{_}If there is ever sunshine I do not recall it; if there are birds they do not sing.
canbe10{_}596{_}There is another dream, another vision of the night.
canbe10{_}597{_}I stand among the shadows in a moonlit road.
canbe10{_}598{_}I am aware of another presence, but whose I cannot rightly determine.
canbe10{_}599{_}In the shadow of a great dwelling I catch the gleam of white garments; then the figure of a woman confronts me in the road--my murdered wife!
canbe10{_}600{_}There is death in the face; there are marks upon the throat.
canbe10{_}601{_}The eyes are fixed on mine with an infinite gravity which is not reproach, nor hate, nor menace, nor anything less terrible than recognition.
canbe10{_}602{_}Before this awful apparition I retreat in terror--a terror that is upon me as I write.
canbe10{_}603{_}I can no longer rightly shape the words.
canbe10{_}604{_}See!
canbe10{_}605{_}they -  Now I am calm, but truly there is no more to tell:  the incident ends where it began--in darkness and in doubt.
canbe10{_}606{_}Yes, I am again in control of myself:  "the captain of my soul."
canbe10{_}607{_}But that is not respite; it is another stage and phase of expiation.
canbe10{_}608{_}My penance, constant in degree, is mutable in kind:  one of its variants is tranquillity.
canbe10{_}609{_}After all, it is only a life-sentence.
canbe10{_}610{_}"To Hell for life"--that is a foolish penalty:  the culprit chooses the duration of his punishment.
canbe10{_}611{_}To-day my term expires.
canbe10{_}612{_}To each and all, the peace that was not mine.
canbe10{_}613{_}III--STATEMENT OF THE LATE JULIA HETMAN, THROUGH THE MEDIUM BAYROLLES  I had retired early and fallen almost immediately into a peaceful sleep, from which I awoke with that indefinable sense of peril which is, I think, a common experience in that other, earlier life.
canbe10{_}614{_}Of its unmeaning character, too, I was entirely persuaded, yet that did not banish it.
canbe10{_}615{_}My husband, Joel Hetman, was away from home; the servants slept in another part of the house.
canbe10{_}616{_}But these were familiar conditions; they had never before distressed me.
canbe10{_}617{_}Nevertheless, the strange terror grew so insupportable that conquering my reluctance to move I sat up and lit the lamp at my bedside.
canbe10{_}618{_}Contrary to my expectation this gave me no relief; the light seemed rather an added danger, for I reflected that it would shine out under the door, disclosing my presence to whatever evil thing might lurk outside.
canbe10{_}619{_}You that are still in the flesh, subject to horrors of the imagination, think what a monstrous fear that must be which seeks in darkness security from malevolent existences of the night.
canbe10{_}620{_}That is to spring to close quarters with an unseen enemy--the strategy of despair!
canbe10{_}621{_}Extinguishing the lamp I pulled the bed-clothing about my head and lay trembling and silent, unable to shriek, forgetful to pray.
canbe10{_}622{_}In this pitiable state I must have lain for what you call hours--with us there are no hours, there is no time.
canbe10{_}623{_}At last it came--a soft, irregular sound of footfalls on the stairs!
canbe10{_}624{_}They were slow, hesitant, uncertain, as of something that did not see its way; to my disordered reason all the more terrifying for that, as the approach of some blind and mindless malevolence to which is no appeal.
canbe10{_}625{_}I even thought that I must have left the hall lamp burning and the groping of this creature proved it a monster of the night.
canbe10{_}626{_}This was foolish and inconsistent with my previous dread of the light, but what would you have?
canbe10{_}627{_}Fear has no brains; it is an idiot.
canbe10{_}628{_}The dismal witness that it bears and the cowardly counsel that it whispers are unrelated.
canbe10{_}629{_}We know this well, we who have passed into the Realm of Terror, who skulk in eternal dusk among the scenes of our former lives, invisible even to ourselves and one another, yet hiding forlorn in lonely places; yearning for speech with our loved ones, yet dumb, and as fearful of them as they of us.
canbe10{_}630{_}Sometimes the disability is removed, the law suspended:  by the deathless power of love or hate we break the spell--we are seen by those whom we would warn, console, or punish.
canbe10{_}631{_}What form we seem to them to bear we know not; we know only that we terrify even those whom we most wish to comfort, and from whom we most crave tenderness and sympathy.
canbe10{_}632{_}Forgive, I pray you, this inconsequent digression by what was once a woman.
canbe10{_}633{_}You who consult us in this imperfect way--you do not understand.
canbe10{_}634{_}You ask foolish questions about things unknown and things forbidden.
canbe10{_}635{_}Much that we know and could impart in our speech is meaningless in yours.
canbe10{_}636{_}We must communicate with you through a stammering intelligence in that small fraction of our language that you yourselves can speak.
canbe10{_}637{_}You think that we are of another world.
canbe10{_}638{_}No, we have knowledge of no world but yours, though for us it holds no sunlight, no warmth, no music, no laughter, no song of birds, nor any companionship.
canbe10{_}639{_}O God!
canbe10{_}640{_}what a thing it is to be a ghost, cowering and shivering in an altered world, a prey to apprehension and despair!
canbe10{_}641{_}No, I did not die of fright:  the Thing turned and went away.
canbe10{_}642{_}I heard it go down the stairs, hurriedly, I thought, as if itself in sudden fear.
canbe10{_}643{_}Then I rose to call for help.
canbe10{_}644{_}Hardly had my shaking hand found the doorknob when--merciful heaven!--I heard it returning.
canbe10{_}645{_}Its footfalls as it remounted the stairs were rapid, heavy and loud; they shook the house.
canbe10{_}646{_}I fled to an angle of the wall and crouched upon the floor.
canbe10{_}647{_}I tried to pray.
canbe10{_}648{_}I tried to call the name of my dear husband.
canbe10{_}649{_}Then I heard the door thrown open.
canbe10{_}650{_}There was an interval of unconsciousness, and when I revived I felt a strangling clutch upon my throat--felt my arms feebly beating against something that bore me backward--felt my tongue thrusting itself from between my teeth!
canbe10{_}651{_}And then I passed into this life.
canbe10{_}652{_}No, I have no knowledge of what it was.
canbe10{_}653{_}The sum of what we knew at death is the measure of what we know afterward of all that went before.
canbe10{_}654{_}Of this existence we know many things, but no new light falls upon any page of that; in memory is written all of it that we can read.
canbe10{_}655{_}Here are no heights of truth overlooking the confused landscape of that dubitable domain.
canbe10{_}656{_}We still dwell in the Valley of the Shadow, lurk in its desolate places, peering from brambles and thickets at its mad, malign inhabitants.
canbe10{_}657{_}How should we have new knowledge of that fading past?
canbe10{_}658{_}What I am about to relate happened on a night.
canbe10{_}659{_}We know when it is night, for then you retire to your houses and we can venture from our places of concealment to move unafraid about our old homes, to look in at the windows, even to enter and gaze upon your faces as you sleep.
canbe10{_}660{_}I had lingered long near the dwelling where I had been so cruelly changed to what I am, as we do while any that we love or hate remain.
canbe10{_}661{_}Vainly I had sought some method of manifestation, some way to make my continued existence and my great love and poignant pity understood by my husband and son.
canbe10{_}662{_}Always if they slept they would wake, or if in my desperation I dared approach them when they were awake, would turn toward me the terrible eyes of the living, frightening me by the glances that I sought from the purpose that I held.
canbe10{_}663{_}On this night I had searched for them without success, fearing to find them; they were nowhere in the house, nor about the moonlit lawn.
canbe10{_}664{_}For, although the sun is lost to us forever, the moon, full- orbed or slender, remains to us.
canbe10{_}665{_}Sometimes it shines by night, sometimes by day, but always it rises and sets, as in that other life.
canbe10{_}666{_}I left the lawn and moved in the white light and silence along the road, aimless and sorrowing.
